==PAGE:2==
Abstract
Today, industrial rotating machinery employed in the oil and gas industry, such as centrifugal pumps
and compressors, are complex and technologically advanced machines. High pressures, small seal clear-
ances and high rotational velocities in compressors and pumps ensure high productivity. However, the
rotational velocities are limited by the stability margins of the rotating machinery. Mathematical models
provide the necessary predictive capabilities of these stability margins, and are commonly based on ﬁnite
element models, i.e. rotordynamic force models, where components like bearings, impellers, and seals
are modelled individually and added to the rotor model. It is well known that the rotordynamic forces
generated by the interaction of process ﬂuid, seal rotor, and seal stator are of primary importance for
the rotordynamic stability and thereby operability and life span of rotating machinery. This renders seal
dynamics and its predictions the overlying topic of this master’s thesis.
The speciﬁc objective of this thesis was to derive a theoretical model for prediction of dynamic forces
in smooth plain annular (gas) seals. The theoretical model is known as the bulk ﬂow model for incom-
pressible ﬂows and should be validated by experiments on a provided test-rig.
The system’s dynamic forces are identiﬁed by obtaining the experimental frequency response functions
and setting them equal to the associated theoretical frequency response functions. Then the stiﬀness and
damping terms can be isolated from the separated equations. The experimental frequency response is
obtained via a SIMO procedure, which consists of perturbation of the global system with a known force
and measuring the resulting displacements.
The theoretical prediction of the dynamic forces is accomplished by obtaining the pressure distribution
of the perturbed system, integrating it over the rotor surface, and performing a least squares ﬁt on a seal
reaction force model. The theoretical model for the prediction of the perturbed pressure is obtained by
reducing the Navier-Stokes equations to the bulk ﬂow equations, performing a ﬁrst order perturbation
expansion, and implementing a numerical solver that solves the coupled system of diﬀerential equations.
The end of the project was ﬁnalized with a comparison between the experimentally obtained dynamic
forces, associated with the test-rigs annular gas seal, and the theoretical predictions. Unfortunately,
these did not agree in trends nor magnitude, and thus, it would seem that the present incompressible
bulk ﬂow model can not accurately describe the eﬀects of an annular gas seal.
The experimental dynamic forces are found to be independent of both the external perturbation frequency
and the rotor angular velocity. It is also found, that while the direct terms are constant and very small,
the coupled terms are in the same order of magnitude as the directs terms of the global investigated
system. The coupled terms are also linearly dependent of the pressure diﬀerence over the seal. Even
though the damping is speciﬁed, the values are only best guesses. The global system is critically damped
and has a limited linear range. The extraction could, therefore, not be conducted around resonance.
The obtained stiﬀness, however, seems to be a good estimate, since the linear range could clearly be seen
during the data extraction.
i
==PAGE:3==
Resumé
Industrielt roterende maskineri, såsom centrifugalpumper og kompressorer, der f.eks. anvendes i olie- og
gasindustrien, er i dag både komplekse og teknologisk avancerede. Høje tryk, smalle tætningsfrirum
og høje rotationshastigheder anvendes for at sikre en høj produktivitet.
Rotationshastighederne er
dog begrænsede af de roterende maskineri’s stabilitetsgrænser, men disse kan forudsiges ved brug af
matematiske modeller. De matematiske modeller er normalt baserede på FE-modeller, dvs. modeller
baseret på rotordynamiske kræfter, hvor komponenterne, så som lejer og tætninger, bliver modelleret
individuelt, for siden at blive tilføjet til rotor modellen. De rotordynamiske kræfter, der genereres af
interaktionen mellem procesﬂuidet, tætningsrotoren og tætningsstatoren er som kendt afgørende for den
rotordynamiske stabilitet og derved funktionaliteten og levetiden for det roterende maskineri. Derfor er
tætningsdynamikken og dens forudsigelser specialets hovedemne.
Det speciﬁkke mål for projektet er at udlede en teoretisk model til forudsigelse af de dynamiske kræfter
i glatte ringformede gastætninger. Den teoretisk anvendte model er kendt som ’bulk ﬂow’-modellen for
inkompressibelt ﬂow. Modellen valideres ved brug af eksperimenter på en givet teststand.
Systemets dynamiske kræfter identiﬁceres ved at ﬁnde de eksperimentelle overføringsfunktioner og sætte
dem lig med de tilhørende teoretiske overføringsfunktioner. Derefter kan stivhed- og dæmpningstermerne
blive isoleret fra de separerede ligninger. De eksperimentelle overføringsfunktioner ﬁndes via en SIMO-
procedure, hvilket indebærer at forstyrre det globale system med en kendt kraft og samtidig måle de
resulterende forskydninger.
Den teoretiske forudsigelse af de dynamiske kræfter opnås ved at ﬁnde trykfordelingen af det perturberede
system, integrere det over rotorens overﬂade, og bruge mindste kvadraters-metoden på en tætnings-
reaktionskraft-model. Den teoretiske model, som forudsiger perturberingstrykket, opnås ved at reducere
Navier-Stokes-ligningerne til bulk ﬂow-ligningerne, udføre en første ordens perturberings ekspansion og
udvikle en numerisk løser, som kan løse det koblede system af diﬀerentialligninger.
Projektet afsluttes med en sammenligning af de eksperimentelt opnåede dynamiske kræfter, der er for-
bundet med forsøgsopstillingens ringformede gastætning og de teoretiske forudsigelser. Desværre er de
eksperimentielle- og teoretiske kræfter ikke enige i trends eller størrelsesorden, hvorfor det kan kon-
kluderes, at den nuværende inkompressible bulk ﬂow-model ikke kan beskrive virkningerne af en glat
ringformet gastætning.
Det viste sig, at de eksperimentelle dynamiske kræfter er uafhængige af både forstyrrelses frekvens og
rotorens vinkelhastighed. Derudover var de direkte koeﬃcienter for gastætningen konstante og meget
små, hvorimod de koblede koeﬃcienter er i samme størrelsesorden som de directe koeﬃcienter af det
globale undersøgte system. De koblede koeﬃcienter er også lineært afhængige af trykforskellen henover
tætningen. Selvom dæmpningen er angivet, er værdierne kun et skøn. Det globale system er kritisk
dæmpet og har et begrænset lineært område, hvorfor værdierne ikke kunne ﬁndes omkring resonans.
Den opnåede stivhed burde dog være et godt bud, da det lineære område tydeligt kunne ses.
ii
==PAGE:4==
Preface
This project was made in the period from the 22th of August 2016 to the 5th of March 2017 as part of
our master’s thesis. The project was conducted at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU-Lyngby)
in the laboratory located in building 414 on a newly constructed test-rig. The work conducted in this
master’s thesis corresponds to 2 x 30 ECTS points and is mandatory for obtaining the degree ’Master
of Science in Engineering Design and Applied Mechanics’.
During the project, we learned how to perform experiments related to vibrations, and in the process,
we became acquainted with the technology and issues related to both active magnetic bearings and
plain annular seals. Furthermore, as part of the mathematical modelling, new areas of expertise were
developed. Speciﬁcally, modern lubrication theory and computational ﬂuid mechanics - which were both
outside of our former skill set.
Even though the theoretical model derived in this project could not correctly describe the plain annular
gas seal characteristics, the documentation in this report should make it easy for future students to
understand the bulk ﬂow theory, and thus be able to implement changes, so that it, hopefully, will work
for plain annular gas seals in the future.
In connection with the project, a special thanks must be given to our PhD supervisor, Jonas Skjødt
Lauridsen. His guidance with relations to electrical engineering and control technology has been essential
for the good start-up of the project and the experimental work.
Furthermore, a special thanks must be given to our supervisor, Ilmar Ferreira Santos, who has been
very helpful and a good guide during the entire project. His guidance, experience, and knowledge in the
ﬁeld of rotordynamics, experimental methods, and common pitfalls has contributed signiﬁcantly to the
success of the project.
Frank Skovsgaard
Date
Ari Lamhauge
Date

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
The following chapter serves as an introduction to the thesis, which will focus on theoretical (for predictive
capabilities) and experimental (for predictive validation) characterisation of dynamic forces in plain
annular gas seals. Firstly, the background and objectives of the project will be presented. Secondly,
a typical mathematical formulation will be presented, along with the generic identiﬁcation procedures
used when obtaining the rotordynamic characteristics both theoretically and experimentally. Lastly, the
chapter is ﬁnalized with a presentation on the structure of the report.
1.1
Background
Today, industrial rotating machinery employed in the oil and gas industry, such as centrifugal pumps
and compressors, are complex and technologically advanced machines. High pressures, small seal clear-
ances and high rotational velocities in compressors and pumps ensure high productivity. However, the
rotational velocities are limited by the stability margins of the rotating machinery.
Generally, mathematical models provide the necessary predictive capabilities of these stability margins,
among others. Rotordynamic modelling is commonly based on ﬁnite element models, i.e. rotordynamic
force models, where bearings, impellers, and seals are modelled individually and added to the rotor model.
For the global rotordynamic model to be capable of representing the behaviour of the rotordynamic
system, it is of importance that the individual components are modelled with high accuracy. This calls
for component level investigations, aimed at assessing the precision with which the dynamic behaviour of
the individual components can be predicted. To assess the applicability of the component level models, it
is common practice to validate these models by comparing theoretical results with experimental results.
It is well known that the rotordynamic forces generated by the interaction of process ﬂuid, seal rotor,
and seal stator are of primary importance for the rotordynamic stability, and thereby operability and
life span of rotating machinery. This renders seal dynamics and its predictions, the overlying topic of
this master’s thesis.
1.2
Objectives
• Derivation of a theoretical model for prediction of dynamic forces in plain annular seals, during
diﬀerent operating conditions.
Speciﬁcally, the theoretical predictions should be made using a
bulk ﬂow model for incompressible ﬂow in plain annular seals, with a seal-rotor performing lateral
motion about the seal centre.
• Experimental characterisation of dynamic forces in a plain annular seal during diﬀerent operating
conditions.
• Validation of the theoretical model, by comparing the theoretical and experimental dynamic forces.
1.3
Characterisation of Rotordynamic Seal Characteristics
The governing equation of motion for a seal-rotor performing small lateral motion about the seal centre
can be described by the reaction forces acting on the rotor from the ﬂuid as:[1]
−
FX
FY

=
 CXX
CY X
−CXY
CY Y
  ˙X
˙Y

+
 KXX
KY X
−KXY
KY Y
 X
Y

(1.1)
In the above equation, C is the damping terms and K the stiﬀness terms. Furthermore, subscript XX
and Y Y denote the direct terms, while XY and Y X denote coupled terms. Note that the added inertia
terms have been neglected, since it has been proven to be insigniﬁcant[1] for gas seals. Additionally,
unlike annular liquid seals, the direct terms are expected to be insigniﬁcant and perhaps negative as well
[1]. However, for the sake of generality, the direct terms will not be neglected.
1
==PAGE:14==
The idea behind the theoretical prediction of the dynamic forces is to obtain the pressure distribution
of the perturbed system, integrating it over the rotor surface and performing a least squares ﬁt on
equation 1.1. The theoretical model for prediction of the perturbed pressure is obtained by reducing the
Navier-Stokes equations to the bulk ﬂow equations, performing a ﬁrst order perturbation expansion, and
numerically solving the system of coupled diﬀerential equations.
The idea behind the experimental identiﬁcation of the dynamic forces is to obtain the experimental
frequency response functions and setting them equal to the associated theoretical frequency response
functions. The stiﬀness and damping terms can then be isolated from the separated equations. The
experimental frequency response is obtained via a SIMO procedure, which consist of perturbation in the
global system with a known force, and measuring the resulting displacements.
1.4
Structure of the Report
Chapter 2 presents the test rig that is going to be used during experiments and deﬁnes parameters
that are important for both the theoretical and experimental work. Furthermore, a preliminary system
investigation is made to ensure that the experimental work is performed in ranges where non-linearities
do not aﬀect the results.
Chapter 3 describes in detail the experimental methods used to obtain the dynamic characteristics
during diﬀerent operating conditions. The methods are described with experimental results to provide a
good understanding of the method. Furthermore, it describes how the active magnetic bearings (AMB)
forces-current factors were obtained. A mathematical model is introduced, which describes a rigid rotor
with springs and dampers located at the active magnetics bearing coordinates, also referred to as AB.
Lastly, a small experimental procedure is presented for deﬁning the pre-swirl as function of the pressure
diﬀerence along with the loss and recovery factors. These variables are used when solving the bulk ﬂow
model.
Chapters 4 to 6 relate to the theoretical prediction of dynamic forces in plain annular (gas) seals.
Chapter 4 deﬁnes the bulk ﬂow model from the Navier-Stokes equations by Reynolds decomposition,
order of scale analysis, and integration across the ﬁlm thickness. Chapter 5 presents the zeroth and ﬁrst
order perturbation approximations of the dimensionless bulk ﬂow equations. It furthermore deﬁnes a
method for obtaining the pressure and presents a relation between these and the dynamic forces. Chapter
6 presents a possibility for solving the zeroth and ﬁrst order equations via the ﬁnite diﬀerence method,
which was chosen due to its implementation possibilities across numerical platforms. Lastly, obtained
numerical solutions are benchmarked against a nominal solution by Childs, which present excellent
agreement.
Chapter 7 presents the experimentally and theoretically obtained dynamic forces of the physical plain
annular gas seal along with the validation of the theoretical bulk ﬂow model.
Chapter 8 discusses the results of the theoretical and experimental work along with the possible dis-
crepancies between the results.
Chapter 9 is a list of suggestions and recommendations for future work, related to both the experimental
modelling and improvements of the test rig.
Chapter 10 presents the conclusion, which summarizes the report as a whole, drawing inferences from
the entire process, as to what has been found or decided and the impact of those ﬁndings or decisions.
2
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 2
The Physical System
In the following chapter, an overview of the experimental test facility is provided. A picture of the test rig,
with labels for components and locations, can be seen in ﬁgure 2.1. The physical system, which the plain
annular seal (AS) is mounted onto, has two radial active magnetic bearings (AMB A & B). The bearings
support the rotor assembly, which is connected to a motor via a ﬂexible coupling (FC), an intermediate
shaft pedestal (ISP), and a drive belt. Furthermore, reference frame B1 is indicated by (X,Y,Z). Please
note that the linear seal characteristics are extracted from the principle of superposition, thus requiring
a linear system response. A good understanding of the system behaviour and its limitations is therefore
required, so that the identiﬁcation is performed correctly and under valid conditions. This chapter is,
therefore, ﬁnalized with a preliminary system investigation.

2.2
The Rotor Assembly
The rotor assembly is shown in ﬁgure 2.3 and consists of two main components, i.e. the rigid rotor and
the two mounted AMB rotors, which are mounted onto the rotor through a conical interference ﬁt and
a locknut. The AMB rotors are made of lamination sheets that are stacked in between an inner and an
outer rotor sleeve, which are held together with a bolt connection. The outer sleeve includes the target
of displacement measurements and the backup bearing surface. ’The rotor is designed to be rigid in
the entire operational range of the test facility and the ﬁrst natural frequency corresponding to the ﬁrst
ﬂexible mode is determined theoretically and experimentally to be at 550 [Hz]’ [page 45][2].

2.3
Annular Seal Assembly
The annular seal assembly seen in ﬁgure 2.4 consists of two untapered smooth seal lands, where the
conﬁguration is symmetrical around the rotor at centre of gravity, CG. The annular seal has four injection
nozzles, which are mounted 90◦apart, ensuring even pre-swirl inside the seal entrance. They are mounted
so that the inlet pre-swirl is tangential to the inlet cavity surface. The inlet seal pressure, and thereby
mass ﬂow rate, is regulated upstream from the seal using a balanced valve dome loaded pressure regulator
charged with air at a speciﬁc pressure (IVP type K32). Four ball valves are used to control the airﬂow
supplied to the individual injection nozzles. In this project, all ball valves are either fully closed or fully
open.

2.4
Active Magnetic Bearings Assembly
The test rig has two radial active magnetic bearings (AMB A & B) with eight poles each. The AMB’s
are located at position A and B, and control the displacement of the rotor relative to the stator via the
generated magnetic forces. One of the active magnetic bearings can be seen in ﬁgure 2.5. As can be seen,
it consists of a number of electromagnets positioned around the rotor position, where two electromagnets
form one electromagnetic couple. The rotor and the electromagnetic couples form four magnetic circuits,
generating attracting forces on the rotor to maintain the position of the rotor relative to the stator. Both
active magnetic bearings can generate forces in reference frame B2, i.e. forces on the rotor in ±x and
±y. If the shown electromagnetic circuit is engaged, a negative force will be generated in the y-direction.
Engaging the right lower electromagnetic couple will generate a positive force in the x-direction, and
so on. Two counteracting magnetic circuits are operated in a bearing magnet, which makes it possible
to generate both positive and negative forces. The counter acting magnetic circuits are controlled via
a diﬀerential driving mode, which means that one magnetic circuit is driven with the sum of the bias
current, i0, and control current, ix, and the opposing one with the diﬀerence. For more information on
AMBs, see [3].
Figure 2.5: Rotor assembly (modiﬁed picture from [2])
An electronic control system consists of ampliﬁers, sensors, and a controller that adjusts the power
supply, and thereby, the magnetic forces generated. Furthermore, the controller is programmed in such a
way, that it allows for perturbation signals to be added to the control signals that are sent to the AMBs.
Thus, perturbation can be performed without external equipment. The active magnetic bearings can be
controlled via a dspace operator interface called ControlDesk, which also provides live sensor readings of
displacement and control currents etc. At the moment, the following signals are available: sine, chirp,
PRBS, and sweep signals. New algorithms can easily be implemented through Simulink. The data is
recorded through Matlab.
2.5
Drive System Assembly
As can be seen on ﬁgure 2.1, an asynchronous motor is connected to the rotor via a ﬂexible coupling
(FC), an intermediate shaft pedestal (ISP), and a drive belt. Angular contact ball bearings supporting
the intermediate shaft housed in the ISP compensate for axial forces acting on the rotor. The motor
speed is controlled via the ControlDesk interface. The point of a ﬂexible coupling is to reduce the added
stiﬀness, so that the rotor will be able to move more freely.
6
==PAGE:19==
2.6
System Conﬁgurations
Throughout the experimental work, the following system conﬁgurations and notations will be used when
working on diﬀerent system conﬁgurations. As can be seen from table 2.3, a total of three system conﬁg-
urations will be investigated, where the ﬁrst is only used in the preliminary system investigation. System
two/three will be used during the experimental identiﬁcation procedure. System two does not include
any seal eﬀects, while system three introduces pressure and airﬂow through the seal, thus including the
seal eﬀects.
System
Included Components
1
Rotor + AMB’s
2

Table 2.3: System conﬁgurations
2.7
Preliminary System Investigation
The ﬁrst investigation is to ﬁnd a linear range, so that the identiﬁcation of the dynamic characteristics can
be carried out with conﬁdence in the experimental assumptions. A linear system will, with multiplication
of a factor onto the input signal, respond to the same factor multiplied with the output signal, and thus
the frequency response function will be the same. Mathematically this can be written as:
H = (factor · output)/(factor · input)
(2.1)
Figure 2.6 shows a plot of the normalized FRF for three diﬀerent perturbation currents, while the only
system eﬀects engaged are those of the AMBs and the control system. As can be seen, the frequency
response function is not the same for all applied external signals.
The FRF shows that the system
response is non-linear from approximately 0-4 Hz (low frequency domain) and 35-100 Hz (high frequency
domain). The reason for the non-linear range in the low frequency domain is unknown but assumed
to be caused by the integrator. The reason for the non-linear range in the high frequency domain is
due to the ampliﬁers being too slow to respond to the present situation, i.e. that the correct reaction
control current will not be available at the time of need. Thus, it is recommended not to extract dynamic
characteristics in these ranges. The experimental identiﬁcation procedure will, therefore, be performed
in the approximate range of 4-35 Hz, where the system behaves linearly.

CHAPTER 3
Experimental Methods
In the following chapter, the experimental method will be summarized. A conceptual presentation will
show how to identify the global and seal dynamic characteristics. Furthermore, a mathematical model
will be presented, to which the experimental data will be ﬁtted. Finally, the process of experimentally
determining the pre-swirl velocity, loss factor and recovery factor will be presented.
3.1
Characterization of Dynamic Forces of Global System
In the following section, the procedure for obtaining a set of experimental dynamic characteristics will
be outlined, while the individual steps will be further explained in the following subsections. Figure
3.1 shows a ﬂowchart of the experimental procedure and consists of four steps: signal generation, signal
processing, model ﬁtting, and post processing. As the chart shows, the signal generation consist of ﬁve
individual experiments where data is recorded. This includes one for each perturbation direction in order
to generate the frequency response functions and one without perturbation, which is used to remove the
unbalanced response from the displacement signal.

Please note that the ﬁgures presented in the following subsections are for a pressure diﬀerence of 0.5 [bar]
over the seal, a shaft rotational velocity of 400 [RPM], and a chirp perturbation current amplitude of
{i} = {0.1 0 0 0}T [A] - with most of the examples being for direction Ax (both force and displacement).
The data is denoted ’example case’.
Set Perturbation
The perturbation consists of a linear chirp signal, which is a sinusoidal signal with a linearly increasing
external frequency and with a constant current magnitude. The data is recorded for each perturbation
direction and saved for later processing. The perturbation direction and current magnitude are controlled
via {i} = {iA,x iA,y iB,x iB,y}T , which is supplied to the active magnetic bearings. However, in order to
perform a SIMO frequency response function, the perturbation should only be activated in one direction
at a time, as indicated in ﬁgure 3.1 via {i} = {0.1 0 0 0}T [A] etc.
Smoothing ’Noisy’ Data
Filters are data processing techniques that can smooth out high-frequency ﬂuctuations or remove un-
wanted periodic trends with a speciﬁc frequency. The ﬁltering is implemented using a Matlab function,
’ﬁlter.m’, and by using a 5th-order Butterworth low-pass ﬁlter with a cut-oﬀfrequency of 0.99Fs/2 =
1584 [Hz].
Removing Unbalance Response
The following subsection explains how the unbalanced responses are removed from the ﬁltered displace-
ment signals. The unbalance, due to uneven distribution of masses around an axis of rotation, induces
large displacements which are independent of the applied perturbation signal. The lacking dependency
will result in an incorrect frequency response function and therefore introduce an error to the experi-
mental dynamic characteristics. Figure 3.2 shows an example of an applied perturbation signal and the
measured displacement signal. The ﬁgure shows that the displacement is dominated by something other
than the external chirp frequency. The displacement is large compared to previously observed amplitudes
when using a similar signal with no rotor rotational velocity, i.e. no unbalance response.

The ﬁrst countermeasure to the unbalance response is the averaged amplitude per angle subtraction. In
this method, the averaged amplitude per rotor angle (without perturbation) is obtained and subtracted
from the ﬁltered displacement signals with perturbation applied. Figure 3.3 shows a time sample of an
unbalanced displacement signal in one direction and the measured rotor angles. Averaging each amplitude
at each rotor angle will result in the third plot in ﬁgure 3.3. The third plot is obtained from a longer
time signal - of 100 [s] - and it can be seen that the standard deviation is quite small. This indicates
that the averaged displacement is a good measure of the unbalance response at each rotor angle. The
method works well, and the coherence increases signiﬁcantly compared to using the displacement signals
directly obtained from each perturbation signal (with the unbalance response included).

The second countermeasure against the unbalance response, which is performed after the ﬁrst counter-
measure, is to evaluate the Fast Fourier Transformation, FFT, of the signal, i.e. evaluating if the signal
still has frequencies and associated magnitude peaks that are inconsistent with that of an applied chirp
signal. If such frequencies are found, a theoretical signal is created via a superposition of sine signals
with the unbalance magnitudes, frequencies, and phases found from the FFT analysis.
The created
signal is then subtracted from the displacement signal at each time step. An example of an amplitude
and frequency couple that is not a part of the chirp signal is indicated via a black circle at f ≈10 [Hz]
on ﬁgure 3.4. This peak should be subtracted from the signal along with the other approximately ten
outliers that can be seen. However, only the largest peaks will be subtracted, since a lot of manual work
will be needed to purify the signal further.

the perturbation force is applied in one direction at a time in its own reference frame, it is natural to
transform the displacements from the global reference frame, B1, to the local reference frame, B2. Below,
the transformation of the position vectors from one reference frame to another is given in analytical form:

Perturbation Current to Forces
The perturbation current signals are converted to the perturbation forces, supplied by the active magnetic
bearings, by multiplying experimentally obtained force-current factors with the perturbation current
signal.
{F} = {ki}{i}
(3.4)

given as:
{ki} = {178.1 185.5 206.3 215.4}T
[N/A]

Frequency Response Function
At this point, the displacement and perturbation signals are identiﬁed in the same reference frame. The
data can now be used to estimate the frequency response function through a SIMO frequency response
procedure.
A modiﬁed version of the Matlab routine, ’frf_general.m’, provided by Ilmar Santos is
used, which generates a table including the perturbation frequency, Frequency Response Function, and
coherence. As an example, an input and output signal is inserted in the ’frf_general.m’ routine, yielding
a frequency response function. This is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.7, where a block length of 15 [s] is used. 12
blocks are used in each experiment.

Extracting Dynamic Characteristics
The idea behind the experimental identiﬁcation procedure is to obtain results for the frequency response
function for both the experimental data and for a theoretical model and setting these equal to each other,
so that [H(ωp)]the = [H(ωp)]−1
exp. Assume a theoretical model on standard form, where a harmonic force
is applied in A or B and the resulting displacements are measured in D or E. The model can then be
reduced and factorized, yielding the theoretical frequency response [N/m]:
[M]{¨qDE} + (ω[G] + [C]){ ˙qDE} + [K]{qDE} = {fAB}
⇒−ω2
p[M] + jωp(ω[G] + [C]) + [K] = {fAB}{ADE}−1 = [H(ωp)]the
(3.6)
Setting the experimental frequency response function, [m/N], equal to the assumed theoretical model
results in the following equations:
[H(ωp)]the = [H(ωp)]−1
exp
⇒−ω2
p[M] + [K] + jωp(ω[G] + [C]) = ℜ([H(ωp)]−1
exp) + jℑ([H(ωp)]−1
exp)
⇒[K(ωp)] = ℜ([H(ωp)]−1
exp) + ω2
p[M]
and
[C(ωp)] = ℑ([H(ωp)]−1
exp)ω−1
p
−ω[G]
(3.7)
Figure 3.8 shows the extracted direct stiﬀness and damping as a function of the perturbation frequency
for the example case. The ﬁgure shows that the stiﬀness is not constant in the beginning nor in the
end of the frequency domain. The non-constant stiﬀness is due to non-linearities and will be further
discussed in section 8. The mathematical model is linear and will, therefore, not be able to describe
the non-linear tendencies. As the model does not apply at these extremities, the stiﬀness will only be
extracted in the linear range, speciﬁcally, at a chosen range of 6-10 [Hz]. The same goes for the damping
+ gyroscopic eﬀect due to shaft rotation. A negative damping coeﬃcient is predicted by the model in
the same range where the non-linearities were found, these results can therefore not be trusted either.

Post Processing
The ﬁnal step is to transform the dynamic forces (equation 3.7) obtained from the experimental method
(qDE,fAB) into dynamic forces acting in the centre of gravity (where the seal is located).
This is
accomplished by ﬁrst doing a transformation to the bearing coordinates (qAB,fAB), secondly, by imposing
the AB form on the matrices (see appendix A), and ﬁnally by transforming the resulting matrices to the
centre of gravity (qCG,fCG). See section 3.4 to ﬁnd the transformation matrices and relations.
3.2
Characterization of Dynamic Forces in Annular Gas Seal
The previous section described how to obtain the dynamic characteristics of the global system (system
1, system 2 or system 3) at one operation condition. However, the dynamic forces of the global system
are not of interest in this project, but only the dynamic forces of the annular gas seal.
Assuming
that the system response is linear, and that superposition of solutions applies, the seal characteristics
are determined as the diﬀerence in global characteristics with and without airﬂow through the seal.
The system characteristics without airﬂow were previously deﬁned as system 2, whereas with ﬂow were
denoted system 3. Accordingly, the dynamic forces of the annular gas seal are deﬁned as:
[K]AGS = [K]3 −[K]2
[C]AGS = [C]3 −[C]2
(3.8)
Figure 3.10 shows the global direct stiﬀness and damping coeﬃcients in CG-coordinates for system 2
and 3 as a function of the perturbation frequency, ωp, with operating conditions as speciﬁed in ’example
case’. Note that the shown global direct dynamic coeﬃcients change insigniﬁcantly from system 2 to 3.
Thus, the direct terms associated with the annular gas seal, are small and negligible.

3.3
Active Magnetic Bearing Forces
According to [3, p. 93], the force on a rotor from two counteracting magnets, controlled via a diﬀerential
driving mode at an operating point, can be written in the following linearised form:
fx(x, i) = kiix −ksx
(3.9)
fx describes the net force acting on the rotor, ki indicates the force-current factor, ks indicates the
force-displacement factor, ix indicates the control-current, and x indicates the rotor displacement [3]. In
this project, the force-displacement factor is seen as a part of the eﬀective stiﬀness of system 1 and 2.
Therefore, this factor is not needed. The AMB forces are, therefore, found using a reduced form of the
linearised AMB force model on the form:
fx(x, i) = kiix
(3.10)
Thus, the conversion from the perturbation current signals to the perturbation forces, supplied by the
active magnetic bearings, is done by multiplication of force-current factors with the perturbation current
signal. The factors were obtained via the experimental setup in ﬁgure 3.12. As the ﬁgure shows, the
rotor-AMB system was loaded with two equal masses using a string-truss system in order to remove
possible moments.
Recording the change in the control currents to the active magnetic bearings at
steady state yields a good indication of these factors. The experiment is analogous to how one would
obtain the spring stiﬀness by loading it with a small force and measure the displacement.
Trusses
Wire
Masses
Attachment
Figure 3.12: Static loading and estimation of linearised force-current factors ki
Figure 3.13(top) shows the applied force as a function of the change in the control-current. The squares,
circles, etc. indicate the average measurement along with two times the standard deviation. The ﬁgure
shows that the force-current curves are approximately linear. The gradient of the curves will be denoted
ki and are shown in the bottom graph. It can be seen that each perturbation current will have diﬀerent
force-current factors multiplied onto it due to diﬀerent gradients of the force-current curves.

3.4
Mathematical Model
In the following section, the mathematical model, for which the experimental data is ﬁtted to, will be
derived superﬁcially. The mathematical model is derived for a rigid shaft performing small vibrations
around its centred position.
The model has four degrees of freedom at the centre of gravity, i.e.
2
translations and 2 rotations. If no other reference frame is noted, reference frame B2 is used.
Assumptions
• The shaft is considered rigid with all mass in the centre of gravity.
• Isotropic material properties for the shaft.
• Small vibrational amplitudes.
• No deformation or displacement in the Z-direction (axial stiﬀness neglected).
• Calibrated system (gravitational force neglected).
Mathematical Model of Rotor-AMB System
Figure 3.14 shows the mechanical model of a shaft-spring-damper system along with a generic model
of the associated stiﬀness and damping. The model has four degrees of freedom, 2 translations and 2
rotations at and around the centre of gravity, {qCG} = {xCG yCG Γ β}T . Furthermore, a free body

The Experimental Rotor–Bearing Models

the bearing locations A and B. The transformation results in:

3.5
Empirical Bulk Flow Parameters
The aim of the following section, is to provide an overview of how the empirical bulk ﬂow parameters can
be obtained. Firstly, the loss and recovery factors are approximated via a combination of experiments
and theory. Secondly, the pre-swirl velocity will be determined.
Loss and Recovery Factors
The bulk ﬂow equations are presently (shown in the following chapters) under-determined, which means
that there are more unknowns than describing equations. Therefore, two approaches are suggested in
order to obtain approximations to the inlet loss factor and outlet recovery factor.
The ﬁrst approach is based on a series of individual experiments, during diﬀerent operating conditions,
and manually guessing values of the factors. The factors that provide the best general ﬁt should then
be used in the model. This approach requires a lot of experiments, manual work, and time, and chances
are that the found factors are incorrect. Therefore, this approach is not recommended, even though one
could obtain results for both factors.
The second approach is based on a single experiment, an assumption, and an iterative method. The
assumption is that no exit recovery is present (ξe = 1), i.e. that the exit pressure Pe and P(L) are
identical. Figure 3.19 presents the pressure ﬁeld in the situation of the unperturbed system. With a
pressure-sensor located at Pm, the pressure at P(0) can be prescribed. Solving the zeroth order bulk
ﬂow equations with the estimated P(0) and with ξ = 0 will yield the average bulk ﬂow velocity W0.
Using equation 4.3 with Ps = ∆P, P(0), and W0, one can determine the ξ factor. The factor can then
be validated by performing a series of experiments to see if it remains constant.
Ps
Pm
P
Z
Pe
Figure 3.19: Pressure ﬁeld due to inlet loss, friction and outlet recovery

==PAGE:34==
Lower Bound of Pre-Swirl Velocity
The experimental determination of the pre-swirl velocity needed for the bulk ﬂow model is obtained by
performing simple experiments: disconnect the motor from the ISP (see ﬁgure 3.20), set the inlet seal
pressure, open the ball valves, and wait (approximately 1 hour per experiment) for a steady state rotor
angular velocity to be reached. With a bias current of 4 [A], the breaking power exerted from the AMB’s
onto the rotor should be small but present. Thus, the obtained steady state rotor velocity will be a lower
bound of the pre-swirl velocity for the applied pressure.
Disconnected Motor
ISP
Figure 3.20: Pre-swirl experimental setup

CHAPTER 4
Modelling of Fluid Motion in Annular Seals
In the following chapter, a mathematical model, formally known as the incompressible bulk ﬂow model,
will be derived for plain annular seals. The derivation is based on diﬀerential analysis of ﬂuid motion,
Reynolds decomposition, scale analysis, and integration across the ﬁlm thickness. The aim of the diﬀer-
ential analysis of the ﬂuid motion is to obtain a set of diﬀerential equations describing the state inside
an inﬁnitesimal ﬂuid element in the ﬂuid ﬁlm land. The resulting equations are the Navier-Stokes (NS)
equations. The aim of the Reynolds decomposition is to separate the time averaged and ﬂuctuating parts
of a ﬁeld quantity in the Navier-Stokes equations. The resulting equations are the Reynolds Averaged
Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations. The aim of the scale analysis is to simplify the RANS equations by
neglecting small terms. The resulting equations are the Thin Film (TF) equations. The aim of the
integration across the ﬁlm thickness is to obtain velocity terms in the TF equations that need to be
integrated across the ﬁlm thickness and to substitute these terms with the bulk ﬂow primitives. The
resulting equations are the Bulk Flow (BF) equations. Inspiration to the derivation was obtained from
Childs [1], Andrés [4], Szeri [5], and common ﬂuid mechanical techniques. All resulting equations are in
agreement with Childs [1], Andrés [4] and Szeri [5]. Supplementary derivations can be found in appx. C.
4.1
Assumptions
1. Film thickness small (C/R ≪1) and (C/L ≪1).
2. Film curvature is negligible.
3. Fluid is a continuum and is Newtonian.
4. Fluid is incompressible, isoviscous and isothermal.
5. Fluid is ideal and adheres to surfaces.
6. Reynolds stresses of the same order of magnitude.
7. Fully developed homogeneous turbulent ﬂow.
8. Stator and rotor surfaces are stiﬀ.
9. Body forces negligible.
10. No strong recirculation regions.
11. Small rotor eccentricity.
12. Untapered Seal geometry.
13. Seal has uniform pre-swirl.
14. Seal has perfect geometry.

Mechanical Model
Figure 4.1 shows a seal with associated symbols describing the seal geometry. Furthermore, it shows the
reference frames used in the mathematical modelling and during the characterization of dynamic forces
of the seal (next chapter). The primary reference frame for the diﬀerential analysis is B6. The subscript,
B6, is discarded in this chapter, so that B6X = X etc.
"As a consequence of the smallness in ﬁlm thickness, the eﬀects of the ﬁlm curvature are negligible on the
operation of the thin ﬁlm. This assumption allows the prescription of an orthogonal Cartesian coordinate
system (X, Y, Z) on the planes of the lubricant ﬁlm..."[4]. The X-axis is in the circumferential direction,
and the Y-axis is across the ﬁlm thickness. Figure 4.2 shows the geometry of the ﬂow region inside an
annular seal in reference frame B6 with an eccentric rotor. The clearance function is illustrated along
with an arbitrary inﬁnitesimal cube element inside the ﬁlm region.
Figure 4.3 shows an inﬁnitesimal cube element with side lengths dX, dY , and dZ, including indicated
directions of stress components and ﬂows at the element surfaces. A standard ﬂuid mechanics convention
is used when dealing with stresses, so that the ﬁrst index indicates the direction normal to the surface
and the second index the direction of the stress component. In the ﬁgures, the grey vectors are the
Z-components of the stresses at their respective surfaces including the associated expression. Similar
expressions exist for the other directions. Furthermore, the ﬂow directions at the surfaces are indicated
along with their associated expressions.

4.3
Boundary Conditions
The boundary conditions associated with the stator and rotor surfaces due to the no-slip assumption
(assumption 5):
U(X, 0, Z, T) = 0
V (X, 0, Z, T) = 0
W(X, 0, Z, T) = 0
U(X, H, Z, T) = UH
V (X, H, Z, T) = VH
W(X, H, Z, T) = 0
(4.1)
From simple kinematics, the velocity, VH, normal to the rotor surface equals the temporal change in ﬁlm
thickness, H, plus the spatial change (advection) of the ﬁlm thickness due to the lateral motion of the
surface with velocity UH. Furthermore, the boundary conditions associated with the bulk ﬂow equations
are stated as:
˜U(X, 0, T) = Up
(4.2)
Ps −P(X, 0, T) = ρ(1 −ξ)2W(X, 0, T)/2
(4.3)
Pe −P(X, L, T) = ρ(1 −ξe)2W(X, L, T)/2
(4.4)

4.4
Mathematical Model
The aim of this section is to derive the bulk ﬂow equations based on the steps presented below. Only con-
ceptual derivations are shown, as the intermediate algebraic manipulations are long and uninformative.
The steps are:
1. Conservation laws →NS equations.
2. Reynolds averaging →RANS equations.
3. Order of magnitude analysis →Thin ﬁlm equations.
4. Integration across the ﬁlm thickness and reducing →Bulk ﬂow equations.

(4.10)
"This form of the Navier-Stokes equations is probably the most famous set of equations in ﬂuid me-
chanics, and has been widely studied. These equations, with the continuity equation, form a set of four
coupled non-linear partial diﬀerential equations for U, V, W, and P. Unfortunately, they are impossible
to solve analytically, except for the most basic cases... Turbulence is not such a case. Turbulent ﬂows
are characterized by large, nearly random ﬂuctuations in velocity and pressure in both space and time.
Turbulent ﬂows often occur in the limit of high Reynolds numbers. For most turbulent ﬂows, it is not
possible to resolve the vast range of time and length scales, even with powerful computers. Thus, in the
case of turbulence, one solves for a statistical average of the ﬂow properties."[6]
Reynolds Averaging
The classical theory of turbulence modelling represents the ﬂow variables as the superposition of a time
averaged quantity (mean variable) and a ﬂuctuating quantity. This is written as:
P = ¯P + P ′
U = ¯U + U ′
V = ¯V + V ′
W = ¯W + W ′
(4.11)
The time averaging of a function, where T1 is the time scale of the “largest” eddies in the ﬂow and T2 is
the time scale of the “slow” temporal variations of the ﬂow that are not directly induced by turbulence,
can be written as:

(4.12)

equations 4.8-4.10 and time averaging it (known as Reynolds-averaging), will produce the Reynolds
averaged continuity equation along with the tangential, radial, and axial RANS equations respectively:

(4.16)
"Osborne Reynolds derived these equations as early as in 1895. The contribution of the turbulent ﬂow
motion to the mean stress tensor is usually known as the Reynolds stress tensor. This tensor has six
independent components that determine the inﬂuence of the ﬂow ﬂuctuations into the mean ﬂow. Note
that the analysis thus far renders four mean ﬂow equations, but at each spatial point in the ﬂow ﬁeld there
are ten unknown variables, i.e. 1 pressure, 3 ﬂuid velocities, and 6 components of the Reynolds stress
tensor. The diﬀerence between the number of unknowns and the number of available equations makes
a direct solution of any turbulent ﬂow problem impossible. The resulting phenomenological problem of
ﬁnding additional equations or conditions to make up the diﬀerence is known as the closure problem of
ﬂow turbulence. The fundamental problem of turbulence ﬂow modelling is to relate the six Reynolds stress
components to the mean ﬂow quantities in some physically plausible manner."[4, notes08] This topic is,
however, out of the scope of this project. Luckily, the smallness of the ratio between the ﬁlm thickness
and the other physical dimensions results in a signiﬁcant reduction of the RANS equations.
Order of Magnitude Analysis
The major characteristic of a thin ﬁlm ﬂow is the smallness of the ratio between the ﬁlm thickness
compared to the other physical dimensions (assumption 1), i.e. (C/R or C/L ≪1). Based on this,
and assuming that all components of the Reynolds stress tensor are of the same order of magnitude
(assumption 6), a dimensionless procedure and an order of magnitude analysis can reduce the RANS
equation in a thin ﬁlm region to [4, note 8: eq 22-25]:
∂U
∂X + ∂V
∂Y + ∂W
∂Z = 0
(4.17)
ρDU

∂X + µ∂2U
∂Y 2 −ρ∂U ′V ′
∂Y

(4.18)

(4.19)

∂Y
(4.20)
Equation 4.19 states that the pressure is constant in the radial direction. Utilizing this information, the
pressure ﬁeld, P, is now only a function of (X,Z,T). The problem has, therefore, been reduced from a
3D problem to a 2D problem. Thus, this equation will not be further evaluated.
τ Y X and τ Y Z are the total shear stresses in the x and z-direction respectively due to viscous and
turbulence ﬂow eﬀects. "Hirs proposed a bulk-ﬂow theory for modeling turbulence in thin ﬁlm ﬂows.
In essence, the analysis seeks to relate the wall shear stress diﬀerences to the mean ﬂow velocity, where
the mean velocity conveys the meaning of an average velocity across the ﬁlm thickness."[4] The thin ﬁlm
equations are, therefore, reduced so that all ﬂow and pressure variables are bulk primitives.
26

(4.41)
H is a function of parameters in reference frame B7. The axial distance, from the start of the seal domain
to where the centre of rotation and translations are measured (B7), is denoted Zp. For a symmetric
seal-rotor conﬁguration as in this project, Zp is the distance from the start of the seal domain to the
mass centre of the rotor. Furthermore, B7X and B7Y are the translations of the rotor, and B7αX and
B7αY are the rotations deﬁned in reference frame B7.

==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 5
Characterization of Dynamic Forces
In the following chapter, the dimensionless bulk ﬂow model will be used to predict the linear dy-
namic characteristics of a plain annular seal due to whirling motion of the rotor.
Speciﬁcally, the
non-dimensional bulk ﬂow model will be approximated through a straight forward expansion, where the
ﬁrst order equations describe the change from the nominal system (perturbation solution) and thus the
linear dynamic forces due to perturbation. Supplementary derivations can be found in appendix D.
5.1
Added Assumptions
15. Rotor performs small but ﬁnite motion.
16. Rotor performs oscillations about the z-axis, i.e. centre of the seal.
17. Rotor performs cylindrical or conical motion.
18. Steady state operations.
5.2
Perturbation Analysis
In the following section, it is assumed that the perturbation eccentricity is very small (ϵ ≪1), so that the
rotor performs a small but ﬁnite motion (assumption 15) about the z-axis. A ﬁrst order approximation
to equations 4.43-4.45 is wanted and achieved using the method of straight forward expansion, where
one assumes expansions on the form [1, eq: 4.24]:
h = h0 + ϵh1 + O(ϵ2)
p = p0 + ϵp1 + O(ϵ2)
u = u0 + ϵu1 + O(ϵ2)
w = w0 + ϵw1 + O(ϵ2)
ur = ur0 + ϵur1 + O(ϵ2)
us = us0 + ϵus0 + O(ϵ2)
fr = fr0 + ϵfr1 + O(ϵ2)
fs = fs0 + ϵfs0 + O(ϵ2)
(5.1)
ϵ is a small dimensionless parameter that is a measure of perturbation by the eccentricity. In this project,
it is used strictly as a bookkeeping parameter.
Later, ϵ is set equal to unity because the solutions
themselves are a measure of perturbation in eccentricity. Furthermore, the zeroth order solutions (u0, w0
etc.) will describe the nominal solution, and the ﬁrst order solutions (u1, w1 etc.) will describe the
change from the nominal solution. Inserting the perturbation expansions deﬁned in equation 5.1 and
neglecting terms of size O(ϵ2) and higher, results in the following set of perturbation equations that are
used when extracting the zeroth and ﬁrst order equations:

(equation 5.1) are inserted into the Taylor expanded expression to replace the continuous functions w,

It is assumed that the conditions inside the seal are at steady state (assumption 18), and that the rotor
remains centred at all time (assumption 16), while operating conditions are as described in ﬁgure 5.1.
As steady state has been reached and the rotor is centred, variations of the zeroth order dependent
functions in the circumferential direction and time should be zero. These assumptions prescribe that the
following gradients can be set equal to zero during the zeroth order perturbation analysis:

(5.27)
The ﬁrst order equations are 2D, as they all have derivatives in both the x and z-direction and are
coupled. The clearance function has a simple harmonic variation in θ. This can be exploited to simplify
the equation of motion from the general 2D case (arbitrary motion) to the reduced 1D equations presented
in the end of this section. The clearance function is restated below, and new parameters are introduced
to simplify the equation:
h1 = −[x(t) + αy(t)(z −zp)] cos(θ) −[y(t) −αx(t)(z −zp)] sin(θ)
= −xcos(z, t) cos(θ) −ysin(z, t) sin(θ)
(5.28)
Since the clearance function is a harmonic variation in θ, a simple harmonic variation in the dependent
variables can also be expected. The following separation of variables solution is therefore assumed for
the dependent perturbation variables. Subscript cos and sin indicate terms multiplied by cosine and
sine respectively:
h1(z, t, θ) = −xcos(z, t) cos(θ) −y1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
w1(z, t, θ) = w1cos(z, t) cos(θ) + w1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
u1(z, t, θ) = u1cos(z, t) cos(θ) + u1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
p1(z, t, θ) = p1cos(z, t) cos(θ) + p1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
(5.29)
Equation 5.29 should be substituted into the ﬁrst order equations 5.18-5.20, and terms multiplied by
cos(θ) and sin(θ) should be collected. This results in six terms that are required to be zero, since cos(θ)
and sin(θ) cannot be zero for θ ∈[0 : 2π]. The six terms form six real equations and are only a function of
z and t. The equations can be reduced to three complex equations by introducing the following complex
representation of the dependent variables in the complex plane:
h1com(z, t) = −xcos(z, t) −jy1sin(z, t)
w1com(z, t) = w1cos(z, t) + jw1sin(z, t)
u1com(z, t) = u1cos(z, t) + ju1sin(z, t)
p1com(z, t) = p1cos(z, t) + jp1sin(z, t)
(5.30)
34
==PAGE:47==
Back substituting xcos(t) and xsin(t), from equation 5.28, into the separated complex representation of
the clearance function results in the following, where the terms describing translation and rotation are
separated:
h1com(z, t) = −

x(t) + jy(t)

−


(5.31)

α1O(z −zp), and introduce the frequency ratio f = Ω/ω. Then the corresponding separation of variables
solutions for the dependent variables become:
h1com(z, t) = h1O(z)ejfωτt
w1com(z, t) = w1O(z)ejfωτt
u1com(z, t) = u1O(z)ejfωτt
p1com(z, t) = p1O(z)ejfωτt
(5.32)

5.3
Perturbation Reaction Forces and Moments
The aim of the following section is to convert the numerical ﬁrst order perturbation pressure acting on
the ﬂuid into perturbation reaction force components acting on the rotor from the ﬂuid. The numerical
solution to the ﬁrst order equations results in a vector, which contains information about the perturbation
ﬂows and pressure, both in the rotated frame of reference and at each grid point. The ﬁrst order solution
can, for a combination of cylindrical and conical motion, be written as:

5.4
Rotordynamic Coeﬃcients
The governing equation of motion for the annular seal’s reaction forces written in matrix form, in the
stationery coordinates, B7, are:
{F} = [M]{ ¨Z} + [C]{ ˙Z} + [K]{Z}
{F} = −{FX1, FY 1, MY 1, MX1}T
{Z} = {X, Y, αY , αX}T
(5.46)
where {Z} is a vector of the variables describing the motion, [M] the mass matrix, [C] the damping
matrix, [K] the stiﬀness matrix, and {F} the external force.
The matrices are the object of determination in this project, and it is the rotordynamic coeﬃcients inside
these matrices that are sought. The matrix vector, {Z}, will for the general motion, both cylindrical
and conical, have four dependent variables, i.e. two translations and two rotations. Thus, the matrices
will all be 4x4 in size. For the seal-rotor conﬁguration, the matrices will be on the following form:

To determine the dynamic characteristics, the above equation of motion has to be transformed to the
frame of reference, where the numeric frequency response functions are determined. This is done by
substituting the independent motion variables with polar representations (i.e. X = R cos(fωt) etc.),
transforming the forces into polar coordinates (i.e. FX1 = FR1 cos(fωt) + Fθ1 sin(fωt) etc.), and ex-
tracting the frequency response functions multiplied by cosine and sine, respectively. Doing so, while
replacing the dimensional variables with dimensionless ones, the following relation, between the non-
dimensionalized and the dimensionalized dynamic coeﬃcients [1, eq: 4.57], are found and the presented
non-dimensionalized frequency response functions identiﬁed. The non-dimensional frequency response
functions are shown in the following subsections.

Dynamic Characteristics Considering Rotor Cylindrical Motion
To determine the dynamic coeﬃcients related to pure translation of the rotor, set rO = 1 and αO = 0,
determine the frequency responses numerically, and perform a least squares ﬁt to the following theoretical
frequency response functions [8, eq: 61]:
fr1,r = Fr1/(r1OF0) = −(−f 2 ˆ
M + fˆc + ˆK)
fθ1,r = Fθ1/(r1OF0) = −(f ˆC −ˆk)
mr1,r = Mr1/(r1OF0L) = −(−f 2 ˆ

mθ1,r = Mθ1/(r1OF0L) = −(f ˆCϵα −ˆkϵα)
(5.49)
Dynamic Characteristics Considering Rotor Conical Motion
To determine the dynamic coeﬃcients related to pure rotation of the rotor, set rO = 0 and αO = 1,
determine the frequency responses numerically, and perform a least squares ﬁt to the following theoretical
frequency response functions [8, eq: 62]::
fr1,α = Fr1/(α1OF0) = −(−f 2 ˆ
Mαϵ + fˆcαϵ + ˆKαϵ)
fθ1,α = Fθ1/(α1OF0) = −(f ˆCαϵ −ˆkαϵ)
mr1,α = Mr1/(α1OF0L) = −(−f 2 ˆ
Mα + fˆcα + ˆKα)
mθ1,α = Mθ1/(α1OF0L) = −(f ˆCα −ˆkα)
(5.50)

CHAPTER 6
Numerical Implementation
The goal of this chapter is to develop an algorithm that can estimate the dynamic forces based on the ﬁrst
order solutions, and to approximate solutions to the zeroth and ﬁrst order matrix equations, i.e. to ﬁnd
a discrete approximation to the function that satisﬁes relationships between various of its derivatives and
the boundary conditions. A ﬁnite diﬀerence method replaces the derivatives in the diﬀerential equations
by ﬁnite diﬀerence approximations, and then assembles the discrete variables into a global system of
equations. This gives an algebraic system of equations that can be solved numerically.
6.1
Finite Diﬀerence Method
In the following section, theory related to the ﬁnite diﬀerence method will be brieﬂy explained, so that
a numerical algorithm can be implemented. The ﬁrst task will be to ﬁnd discrete approximations to the
diﬀerentials of the matrix equations.
Finite Diﬀerence Approximation
The approximated derivatives are found using Taylor series expansions of a function, f(x), around a
point, xi, resulting in the following equation:
f(x) = f(xi) + f ′(xi)(x −xi) + f ′′(x0)(x −xi)2/2 + O(f ′′′)
(6.1)
A number of options exist when evaluating the function f(x), for example the left sided three point
scheme (LS), central diﬀerence scheme (CD), and the right sided three point scheme (RS). These three
types of ﬁnite diﬀerence schemes will all be used. Figure 6.1 shows which stencil points are used for
the individual schemes. The central diﬀerence scheme uses the centre grid point as xi, along with the
neighbouring grid points, (xi±1), to evaluate f(xi).

6.2
Discretization of Matrix Perturbation Equations
The aim of the following section, is to obtain a discrete formulation of the domain and the matrix
perturbation equations. When done correctly, the continuous functions will be transformed to a simple
algebraic form of the following type, which can be evaluated at each spatial point:
[AG]{sG} = {cG}
{sG} = {s1, ..., sn}T
(6.8)
42
==PAGE:55==
[AG] is the global assembled ﬁnite diﬀerence matrix, containing the diﬀerential approximations and
constants that should be multiplied onto {sG}. {sG} is a global vector containing the dependent variables
evaluated at each spatial point, and {cG} is a vector containing the remaining terms that are independent
of {sG}.
Seal Domain Discretization
The domain is discretized in space, using a mesh with uniform mesh size, so the diﬀerence between
two consecutive grid points will be ∆z, where {z} = {z1, .., zn}T . Figure 6.3, shows the discretized one
dimensional seal domain in the interval z ∈[0 : 1].

The remaining task is to assemble the ﬁnite diﬀerence approximation into the global system of equations.
A conceptual example for a mesh with ﬁve grid points is shown in ﬁgure 6.2, where the zeroth order LS,
CD, and RD matrix stencils are used.
The continuous zeroth order matrix equation is transformed to a simple algebraic form of the following
type, which can be evaluated at each spatial point:
[A0]{s0} = {c0},
{s0} = {u0(1), p0(1), ..., u0(N), p0(N)}T
(6.11)
[A0] is the global assembled zeroth order ﬁnite diﬀerence matrix, containing the diﬀerential approxima-
tions and constants that should be multiplied onto {s0}. {s0} is a global vector containing the dependent
variables evaluated at each spatial point, and {c0} is a vector containing the remaining zeroth order terms
that are independent of {s0}.

following type, which can be evaluated at each spatial point:

and constants that should be multiplied onto {s1}. {s1} is a global vector containing the dependent
variables evaluated at each spatial point, and {c1} is a vector containing the remaining ﬁrst order terms
that are independent of {s1}.
6.3
Pseudo Algorithms
The aim of the following section is to provide an overview of the overall numerical implementation. The
aim of the project is to determine the dynamic characteristics of a plain annular seal during operation.
These can be found from the solutions to the perturbation equations. The function of algorithm 1 is to
derive the dynamic coeﬃcients on the basis of the perturbation equations’ solution, while algorithm 2’s
function is to solve the perturbation equations. The pseudo codes for both algorithms can be seen in
ﬁgures 6.4-6.5, while the actual scripts can be found in appendix E.
Algorithm 1: Predict Dynamic Characteristics Matrix [M], [C], and [K]
Preprocessing
Set nominal seal parameters
Processing - Cylindrical Characteristics
Set r1O = 1 and α1O = 0
For i = 1 : N
Set ratio f(i), f ∈[0 : 2]
Run Algorithm 2 and obtain W0 and {p1}
Calculate frf(i) for f(i) ({fr1(W0, {p1})},{fθ1(i)}(W0, {p1}))
End
Least squares ﬁt (frfnumeric,r = frfmodel,r)
Processing - Conical Characteristics (not implemented)
Set r1O = 0 and α1O = 1
For i = 1 : N
Set ratio f(i), f ∈[0 : 2]
Run Algorithm 2 and obtain W0 and {p1}
Calculate frf(i) for f(i) ({fr1(W0, {p1})},{fθ1(i)}(W0, {p1}))
End
Least squares ﬁt (frfnumeric,α = frfmodel,α)
Figure 6.4: Pseudo code for numerical determination of dynamic coeﬃcients
44
==PAGE:57==
Algorithm 2: Perturbation Solutions for {x0}, {x1}, and W0
Preprocessing:
Discretize seal domain
Processing - Zeroth Order Solution:
Initialize arrays with startguess ({u0,old}, {u0,new}, {p0,old}, {p0,new})
Initialize scalar with startguess (W0,old, W0,new)
While ||{u0,old} −{u0,new}|| or ||{p0,old} −{p0,new}|| is greater than error
Update arrays and scalars ({u0,old}, {p0,old}, W0,old)
Assemble global constant vector ([c0(u0,old, W0,old)])
Assemble global ﬁnite diﬀerence matrix ([A0(u0,old, W0,old)])
Impose boundary conditions
Solve for {s0} = [A0]\{c0} ({u0,new}, {p0,new})
Estimate new guess ({u0,new}, {p0,new}, W0,new)
Processing - First Order Solution:
Assemble global constant vector ([c1(u0,old, W0,old)])
Assemble global ﬁnite diﬀerence matrix ([A1](u0,old, W0,old))
Impose boundary conditions
Solve for {s1} = [A1]\{c1} ({w1,new}, {u1,new}, {p1,new})

Convergence Study
In ﬁnite diﬀerence modelling, a ﬁner mesh typically results in a more accurate solution. However, a ﬁner
grid increases the computational time. Therefore, it is important to ﬁnd a minimum number of grid
points that will ensure converged results. The minimum number of grid points can be determined via a
convergence study. Figure 6.6 shows a convergence study of the circumferential non-dimensional nominal
bulk ﬂow velocity at the seal centre, u0(0.5). Based on this convergence study, it is recommended to
use more than 15 grid points to ensure converged results from the implemented one dimensional ﬁnite
diﬀerence method. In the following numerical simulations, a total of 100 grid points has been used.

Benchmarking of Zeroth Order Algorithm
Childs presents a total of six cases with numerical zeroth order solutions, where the nominal seal presented
in table 6.1 is used, but with small variations in certain parameters. These six plots represent, to some
extent, a parameter study of consequences when varying certain parameters.
A small selection of comparison ﬁgures will be shown, in order to qualitatively determine the discrepancies
between the simulations and Childs’ [1] solutions. All obtained zeroth order results are compared to his
numerical results, see appendix E.
Figure 6.7 and ﬁgure 6.8 are good representations of the overall discrepancy between the zeroth order
simulations and Childs’ solutions.
The discrepancies are lower than 1%, even though the data was
manually extracted using the Matlab function Grabit. The same agreement exists for all but one of the
six cases. However, it can be shown that Childs’ solution does not fulﬁl the boundary condition speciﬁed,
whereas the simulation does. The zeroth order solution algorithm can, therefore, be assumed correctly
implemented and validated.

Benchmarking of First Order Algorithm
Childs presents a total of fourteen cases with numerical ﬁrst order solutions, where the nominal seal
presented in table 6.1 is used, but with small variations in certain parameters. A small selection of these
ﬁgures has been used, with the sole purpose of validating the numerical ﬁrst order algorithm. A larger
selection of obtained dynamic characteristics are compared to Childs’ numerical results, see appendix E.
The ﬁrst order solutions consist of the ﬁrst order axial velocity, circumferential velocity, and pressures as
function of the axial seal location. However, Childs does not show these results directly. Instead, Childs
shows the dynamic characteristics of his nominal seal with varying parameters. Fortunately, these results
are dependent on the ﬁrst order solutions, so, as long as the predicted dynamic characteristics agree with
Childs’ results, the ﬁrst order algorithm can be considered validated.
Figures 6.9-6.11 are good representations of the overall discrepancy between the present simulation and
Childs’ simulation. As can be seen from these ﬁgures, the discrepancy increases with higher ratios of
L/D. However, the maximum discrepancy found is around 1%. The ﬁrst order solution algorithm can,
therefore, be considered correctly implemented and validated.

CHAPTER 7
Results
The following chapter documents the results of both the experimental and theoretical work, which has
been separated due to poor agreement. The chapter will summarize the experimental limitations, identify
the pre-swirl, present some of the experimental tendencies, evaluate the experimental characteristics, and
last, but not least, show that the bulk ﬂow model is not able to obtain valid characteristics for plain
annular gas seals.
7.1
Experimental Limitations
The limitations in table 7.1 are not expected to be associated with the mechanical system, i.e. instabilities
or resonance due to the applied pressure and rotor angular velocity, but due to the control system. The
chosen experimental range is limited to a pressure diﬀerence of [0,1.6] [bar], angular rotor velocity of
[0,600] [RPM], perturbation current amplitude of 0.1 [A], and bias current of 4 [A], since the control
system is operational and stable in this range.

7.2
Pre-swirl
Figure 7.1 shows the experimentally obtained pre-swirl velocities as a function of the pressure diﬀerence.
By sheer luck, an alternate controller, installed before this project was started, was successfully accessed
half-way through the project. This controller was stable in operation, even at pressures that exceeded
the previous deﬁned limits, and was, therefore, used to obtain the pre-swirl at 1.2 [bar]. Unfortunately,
it has been impossible to switch over to this controller ever since. The three experiments (indicated by
dots) show the lower bound estimation of the pre-swirl, which is linearly increasing with the pressure
diﬀerence. It can be seen that the experimental curve starts with an experimental pre-swirl diﬀerent
from zero, which is not physical. However, it makes sense, as the air has to exert a moment onto the
rotor that is greater than the breaking power of the AMB’s before it can start to rotate. Since the
lower bound is linear, it is fair to assume that the estimated pre-swirl is linear as well (see chapter 8 for
discussion on linearity). Imposing that the pre-swirl should be zero at zero pressure diﬀerence, a linear
curve can be made, ranging from (0,0) to the last point of the experimental data, approx. (1.22,11.16).
The gradient of the curve is approximated to be a ≈8.9 [(m/s)/bar], with U0 = a∆P.

7.3
Experimental Tendencies
The results shown in ﬁgure 7.2 are for both system 2 and 3, with ∆P = 0.5 [bar], ω = 400 RPM, and
a chirp perturbation amplitude of {i} = {0 0 0.1 0}T [A]. Please note that these are the only results
that are shown in ’experimental’ coordinates and not CG coordinates. The following tendencies apply
globally for other perturbation directions and operating conditions. The coherence is best when the
displacement and perturbation are in the same direction, and when the measured displacement is closest
to the perturbation source. The FRFs clearly show that no change is present in the direct FRF, while
a signiﬁcant change can be seen in the coupled FRF from system two to three. It can also be seen that
while the direct phases are unaﬀected, the coupled become more continuous (instead of random). Thus,
it should be expected that only the coupled eﬀect will be signiﬁcant when using an plain annular gas
seal.

showed that the coupled stiﬀness and damping coeﬃcients associated with system 2 were almost zero.
The coupled terms of system 2 are, therefore, neglected in the following results, and thus, it is assumed
that all measured coupling eﬀects are solely due to the seal.

7.5
Theoretical Dynamic Forces
The aim of the following section is to show that the present bulk ﬂow code does not work for annular
gas seals. It can be shown that the found tendencies and magnitudes from the experimental work do
not correlate with the prediction of the bulk ﬂow theory. Childs states that the bulk ﬂow codes for plain
annular gas seals perform poorly compared to experiments [1], even when accounting for compressibility.
Thus, the present work simply validates his ﬁndings. Table 7.3 shows the used parameters, which are
based on ﬁndings from literature and experience obtained during operation of the test rig.
