The result section can be ﬁnalized with the conclusion that the implemented bulk ﬂow model and the
experimental dynamic characteristics of annular gas seals do not agree. Thus, the present bulk ﬂow
model cannot correctly model the annular gas seal. However, the model should work for liquids.
56
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 8
Discussion
The following chapter will present several topics that relate to the validity of the obtained results, both
experimentally and theoretically. Sources of errors are investigated, and proposals on how to improve
the test rig are presented.
Incompressible Flow
The present bulk ﬂow model for the annular seal is derived with the assumption of incompressible ﬂow.
In engineering practice, gas ﬂows are considered incompressible when the Mach number is under 0.3 [9,
p. 149]. In ﬂuid dynamics, the Mach number, Ma, is a dimensionless quantity representing the ratio of
ﬂow velocity, u, past a boundary to the local speed of sound, c, of the medium.
Incompressible:
Ma = u/c < 0.3
(8.1)
The speed of sound in air is approximately c = 340 [m/s].
The predicted zeroth order axial bulk
ﬂow velocity is around 200 [m/s], rendering the Mach number Ma ≈0.6, which clearly violates the
incompressibility assumption. Thus, the model is not valid for the annular seal while using gas. When
taking compressibility into account, the model might provide better estimates. However, according to
Childs [1], the compressible version of this model performs as poorly as the incompressible version.
Bias Current Breaking Power
During the experiments for obtaining the pre-swirl velocity as function of the pressure diﬀerence, diﬀerent
controller settings were investigated, in order to see if the experimental range could be increased. It was
noted that an increase in the bias current signiﬁcantly reduced the steady state rotor velocity for the
pre-swirl experiments. An example follows: A pressure diﬀerence of more than 0.7 [bar] was set, and
the rotor was allowed to spin freely. With a bias current of 4 [A], the control system became unstable at
around 1200 [RPM], so the pre-swirl would have been greater than 1200 [RPM]. The same experiment
was then performed for a bias current of 6 [A], which resulted in a steady state rotor velocity of only
600 [RPM]. One of the big beneﬁts with active magnetic bearings is that they should provide (almost)
frictionless rotation. This then seems somewhat countermeasured by the large increase in breaking power
with increasing bias current, which could be needed with higher loads.
Active Magnetic Bearing Forces
Due to lack of time and the length of each experiment, obtained force-current factors (ki) are the result of
only a small portion of experiments. Considering both the average of all the experimental force-current
gradients along with the standard deviations, the force-current factors could be constant and are assumed
to be so. If the experiment was conducted for a longer time period and perhaps with more repetitions,
it could possibly produce more consistent results for the force-current factors, reducing the standard
deviation. This should produce even better experimental frequency response functions, thus reducing
the small error on the experimental dynamic forces. Ideally, an external shaker would be introduced to
the system, so that the external forces would be known.
Pre-swirl
For the numerical approximations, the pre-swirl was assumed to increase linearly with an increasing
pressure, see ﬁgure 7.1. However, from Bernoulli’s equation, the pre-swirl should increase approx. U0 ≈
√
∆P. Experiments were conducted for diﬀerent pressure diﬀerences, and they showed that the pre-swirl
velocity was increasing linearly. The reason behind this is thought to be the limiting pressure range.
If the experiments were conducted for a larger pressure diﬀerence range, it would be expected to show
quadratic tendencies. However, due to the limited range, a linear ﬁt should be suﬃcient. If the stable
pressure range increases in the future, a new study should be made on the pre-swirl tendencies and the
linear assumption should be re-evaluated.
57
==PAGE:70==
Removal of Unbalance Response
The removal of the unbalance response from the displacement signal was done according to standard
methods, which includes subtraction of averaged displacements and a FFT analysis, see chapter 3.
After the removal, the chirp signal was clearly present in the displacement signal. However, the signals
seem quite noisy. This could possibly be solved if more time was put into the signal processing of the
displacement signals via FFT and more post processing.
Non-Linearities and Controller
During the project, there has been a lot of speculation in what is causing the non-linearities and the
control-system failure. Based on limited experience with another pre-installed alternate controller, the
system became more stable - for larger RPM and ∆P. At the time, when it was successfully accessed,
it did not fail. However, it is unknown if the linear range of the system changes with the alternative
controller, and it has been impossible to access it ever since. The increased stability, however, indicates
that a controller can actually make the system more stable - but it is not understood as to why.
Imprecise Pressure Readings
The present Kistler 4065B pressure transducers, mounted ﬂush with the seal land, are industrial scale
high pressure sensors. The pressure sensors are not calibrated for such a low pressure range, as it was
initially expected that the test rig would be operational at high pressures. During the project period,
a master’s student named Peter Juhl Savnik was associated with the project. He got the task of re-
calibrating the pressure sensors for the range [0,4][bar] and found that the precision was approx. 0.5
[bar], with single peaks of up to 4 [bar]. This is far too imprecise at the current operating range and
introduces a large margin of error.
During operation of the test-rig, it was also realised, that the reference pressure readings drifted over
time. This occurred mainly for ’high’ pressures and when reaching instability. This introduces an even
bigger increase in the margin of error.
Sensibility Problems
Due to the nature of damping coeﬃcients, it needs to be identiﬁed as close as possible to resonance,
which is not possible due to non-linearities. Furthermore, due to the size of the seal damping coeﬃcients,
subtraction of the damping without ﬂow from the damping with ﬂow results in a noisy result, rendering
it diﬃcult to identify. A manual extraction is performed in order to obtain the damping coeﬃcients from
within the linear range, where the ﬂuctuations are at their lowest. The results are best guesses.
Block Time and Repetition
The coherence and precision of the dynamic forces could possibly be improved by either increasing the
block time (time it takes to go from 0 - 100 Hz) or by increasing the number of block repetitions. However,
a natural limitation in data capturing process was found at 15 [s] x 12 (i.e. 3 minutes of experimental
time). Too many blocks or too long experiments either froze the operating computer, leaving the AMB
uncontrolled, or resulted in a failure when saving the test-rig signals. The computer or dSpace hardware
are simply not powerful enough to perform long experiments. With 15 [s] x 12 blocks, nearly all signals
could be saved so that both the authors and Jonas Skjødt Lauridsen could use the same datasets for
experimental identiﬁcation. With the minimum required signals related to this project (perturbation
currents, rotor displacement, time, rotor angle, etc.) the block repetitions could be increased to 32 (i.e.
8 minutes of experimental time). However, the results did not improve signiﬁcantly. The only remaining
option would be to increase the block time and reducing the number of blocks. If this improves the
coherence is unknown.
Rotor Speed
The system seems to struggle at maintaining a constant rotational rotor speed.
This increases the
remaining noise after the removal of the unbalance response. Some of the remaining noise is removed by
the FFT, but experience shows that some of the unbalance response still contaminates the displacement
signals.
58
==PAGE:71==
Experiments Without Angular Spin of Shaft
These types of experiments are much simpler, since the unbalance response does not need to be sub-
tracted from each performed experiment. The problem is that the ﬂexible coupling provides dynamics
characteristics that are both dependent on the angular position of the shaft and the pretension of its
bolts. Provided that the coupling stays in the same exact position every day, and that the pretension
of the bolts remains unchanged during the whole experimental period, comparable results could be ob-
tained. However, bolts have a tendency for loosening with vibrating systems, and chances are that the
rotor angular position changes. Thus, the global dynamics of the system without the annular gas seal, is
bound to change noticeably from time to time, rendering the experimental results incomparable. There-
fore, it was chosen to do all experiments with rotor spin, as the global dynamic forces become averaged.
However, it does introduce other diﬃculties like unbalanced responses.
Mass Moment of Inertia
During the experimental work, diﬀerent properties of the rotor shaft were needed. This information
was extracted from SolidWorks.
However, it was noticed that the mass moment of inertia was not
transformed from the output coordinate system (misaligned in the z-direction) to the mass centre. This
is wrong and should yield incorrect experimental results.
The misalignment of the coordinate system is only in the z-direction. The distance between the centre of
gravity and the output coordinate system is 20.1 [mm]. Using Steiner’s parallel axis theorem (equation
8.2), it can be transformed to the centre of gravity. Fortunately, this did not aﬀect the mass moment of
inertia signiﬁcantly, so the obtained dynamic forces are still valid.
IXX,CG = IY Y,CG = IXX,O + m · d2 = 5.23 + 70 · 0.02012 ≈5.25 [kg m2]
(8.2)
59
==PAGE:72==
CHAPTER 9
Future Work
Controller: The present experimental range is limited. It is expected that problems are related to the
controller and not necessarily only to the ampliﬁers. At one point in the project, an alternate controller
was accessed, and the system was quite stable, even for pressure and rotational velocities higher than
the limits of the used controller. However, it has not been possible to switch over to the controller since.
It is recommended to make the alternate controller accessible and to test its performance. It might be,
that it could work for annular gas seal characterizations.
Ampliﬁers: According to Jonas Skjødt Lauridsen, the ampliﬁers react too slow. It is therefore recom-
mended to install new ampliﬁers, so that the experimental range can be expanded.
Factors: One essential requirement for obtaining good and physical results for the dynamic character-
istics is that the supplied external force is known. It is imperative that the force-current factors, ki,
are obtained properly. It is recommended to perform extensive tests to obtain the force-current factors,
so that these will be available with good precision for future projects. Furthermore, the force-current
coeﬃcients could be implemented into the control system, so that a perturbation force can easily be
controlled in [N].
Liquid: Since the present bulk ﬂow code works well for liquids (according to Childs [1]), it would be
natural to switch from gas to liquid.
Manometers: The manometers, currently installed on the airﬂow supply line, have a pressure range
between 0-100 [bar]. Since it is not likely that the pressure will be in that range, it is recommended to add
a low ranged manometer with a cutoﬀand safety valve, so that low pressure readings can be performed
when setting the pressure.
Presently, it is quite diﬃcult to set the same pressure as in a previous
experiment, and thus, one has to redo all experiments for that pressure if something is forgotten.
Breaking: One thing that is rather peculiar is that an increment in bias current results in the rotor
being slowed severely. This was observed when the pre-swirls were investigated. The pressure of this
experiment is unknown, but for a bias current of 4 [A], the pre-swirl was determined to be larger than
1200 [RPM] (limit of RPM). For a bias current of 6 [A], the steady state velocity was around 600 [RPM].
Thus, the active magnetic bearings induced breaking power onto the rotor. It is recommended to ﬁgure
out if this is intended, and possibly how it can be avoided.
Sensor: It is recommended to install an additional pressure sensor in the seal land.
It can be an
arbitrary position inside the seal.
Doing so, will eliminate the loss and recovery factors (ξ and ξe),
rendering no unknowns of the system. Presently, the pre-swirl can be obtained experimentally, and by
doing measurement of the stator and rotor roughness the relative roughness factor can be found.
Loss and Recovery Factors: If the new pressure sensor is implemented, the loss and recovery factors
could be determined directly from the system of equations. It could be interesting to see how constant
these factors remain during diﬀerent operating conditions.
Coupling: Presently, the ﬂexible coupling provides dynamics characteristics that are both dependent
on the angle of rotation and the pretension of its bolts. Since the coupling direct dynamic forces is in the
same order of magnitude as the AMBs, it is important that the coupling characteristic are well known.
It is recommended to perform extensive tests of the coupling, and to provide guidelines for how it should
be fastened, and by how much torque, so that the characteristics can be kept the same in both the X
and Y-direction without rotor spin.
Compressibility: Derive the bulk ﬂow equations for a compressible ﬂow and perform the perturbation
analysis as described in this project. The methods and structure presented in this project hold true, only
the equations will change slightly. Then check if the compressible bulk ﬂow model has a better prediction
of the dynamic coeﬃcients. Note that Childs does, however, concludes that the bulk ﬂow model will not
work for plain annular gas seals.
CFD Models: Perform perturbation expansions of the model used in CFD for turbulent ﬂow, i.e. ’One
equation’ or the ’Two equation’ (k-ϵ model), and perform numerical predictions of the dynamic char-
acteristics. However, expect severe simulation time, model complexity, and numerical implementation
diﬃculties with such a project.
60
==PAGE:73==
CHAPTER 10
Conclusion
The objective of the thesis, was to derive a theoretical model for prediction of dynamic forces in plain
annular (gas) seals. The theoretical model is known as the bulk ﬂow model for incompressible ﬂows and
should be validated by experiments on a provided test-rig.
The test-rig consists of two radial active magnetic bearings that support a rotor. The rotor is connected
to an asynchronous motor via a ﬂexible coupling, an intermediate shaft pedestal, and a drive belt.
Furthermore, a plain annular seal is mounted around the mass centre of the rotor and is supplied with
an even tangential pre-swirl, relative to the stator cavity surface. The experimental test-rig is a product
of a recently ended PhD project, and as such, the test-rig has teething problems that need to be solved in
future work. These problems have limited the operational range (stability issues) and the experimental
identiﬁcation range (non-linear responses).
The global systems dynamic forces are identiﬁed by obtaining the experimental frequency response
functions and setting them equal to the associated theoretical frequency response functions. Then, the
stiﬀness and damping terms can be isolated from the separated equations. The experimental frequency
response is obtained via a SIMO procedure, which consist of perturbation in the global system with a
known force and measuring the resulting displacements. The dynamic forces of the annular gas seal are
obtained by subtracting the dynamic characteristic of the global system with seal eﬀects from the global
system without seal eﬀects. The underlying assumption of the operation is that the system response is
linear - which it fortunately is, for a limited range.
The theoretical prediction of the dynamic forces is accomplished by obtaining the pressure distribution
of the perturbed system, integrating it over the rotor surface, and performing a least squares ﬁt on a seal-
rotor reaction force model. The theoretical model for prediction of the perturbed pressure is obtained by
reducing the Navier-Stokes equations to the bulk ﬂow equations, performing a ﬁrst order perturbation
expansion, and implementing a numerical solver that solves the system of coupled diﬀerential equations.
The implementation of the bulk ﬂow code was validated against Childs’ original numerical solutions for a
nominal seal, which showed excellent agreement, and thus, validating the implementation and derivation
of the incompressible plain bulk ﬂow model.
The end of the project was ﬁnalized with a comparison between the experimentally obtained dynamic
forces, associated with the test-rigs annular gas seal, and the theoretical predictions. Unfortunately,
these did not agree in trends, and thus it would seem that the present incompressible bulk ﬂow model
can not accurately describe the eﬀects of an annular gas seal. These results were therefore separated, so
that the result section is divided between the experimental and theoretical results. Further comments
on the theoretical results will not be presented in the conclusion.
The experimental dynamic forces are found to be independent of both the external perturbation frequency
and the rotor angular velocity. It can also be seen that while the direct terms are constant and very small,
the coupled terms are in the same order of magnitude as the global direct terms. The coupled terms
are also linearly dependent of the inlet pressure. Even though the damping is speciﬁed, the values are
only best guesses. The global system is critically damped and has a limited linear range. The extraction
could, therefore, not be conducted around resonance. The obtained stiﬀness, however, seems to be a
good estimate, since the linear range could clearly be seen during the data extraction.

APPENDIX B
Experimental Algorithm
The following appendix holds algorithms for experimental identiﬁcation of of dynamic forces of the global
system during an operation. The implementation is close to the presented ﬂowchart in ﬁgure 3.1. In
total, 12 algorithms (functions and scripts) are implemented with individual objectives for processing
the signal. Four experiments are evaluated at a time, which it self has four direction. Therefore it has
been chosen to keep everything in cell structures instead of arrays, where each cell block has an array
associated with it. It is more tricky code wise, however much easier to keep track of corresponding
mathematical matrices.

[ dataout ] = preprocessing_dataprep ( nr , Experiments , T_interval , Nrepeat , subtract )

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

% PREPARATION OF EXPERIMENTAL DATA FOR ANALYSIS

APPENDIX C
Derivations of the Bulk Flow Model for Annular Seals
The following appendix is a supplement to chapter 4 of the main report. Please note, that not every-
thing will be further documented, as the information is obtainable from a large selection of literature.
Suggestion to literature will be provided if no further derivations is performed.
C.1
Boundary Conditions
The boundary conditions associated with the stator and rotor surfaces due to the no-slip assumption
(assumption 5):
U(X, 0, Z, T) = 0
V (X, 0, Z, T) = 0
W(X, 0, Z, T) = 0
U(X, H, Z, T) = UH
V (X, H, Z, T) = VH
W(X, H, Z, T) = 0
(C.1)
C.2
Navier-Stokes Equations
More information than what is provided in chapter 4 will not be provided. The Navier-Stokes equations
are well known, and well documented equations. If more information is needed than what is provided in
the main report, refer to literature like [6] [9]. The Navier-Stokes Equations are written as:
∂U
∂X + ∂V
∂Y + ∂W
∂Z = 0

(C.5)

C.4
Thin Film Equations
The RANS equation of motion will be reduce to form the thin ﬁlm equations. The reduction is based
on the work of Szeri [5], and is done via an order magnitude analysis.
Please refer to [5] for more
information, including ﬁgures etc. Introducing dimensionless variables so that the following relationship
between the dimensional and dimensionless variables exist:
(X, Y, Z) = LXZ(x, ϵy, z)

U ∗)t
(U, V , W) = U ∗(u, ϵv, w)
(U ′, V ′, W ′) = V ∗(u′, v′, w′)

p

According to this analysis, mean ﬂow inertia is of same order of magnitude as the surviving Reynolds
stresses and both quantities should be taken into account. In dimensional form, the thin ﬁlm equation
of motion becomes
∂U
∂X + ∂V
∂Y + ∂W
∂Z = 0
(C.28)
ρDU
DT = −∂P
∂X + µ∂2U
∂Y 2 −ρ∂U ′V ′
∂Y
= −∂P
∂X + ∂τ Y X
∂Y
(C.29)
∂P
∂Y = 0
(C.30)
ρDW
DT = −∂P
∂Z + µ∂2W
∂Y 2 −ρ∂W ′V ′
∂Y
= −∂P
∂Z + ∂τ Y Z
∂Y
(C.31)
Note also that the pressure in the radial direction remains constant. Thus this equations will be dropped,
an P is now a function of only (X, Z, T). This reduces the problem from a 3D to a 2D problem.
τ Y X and τ Y Z are the total shear stresses in the x and z-direction respectively due to viscous and
turbulence ﬂow eﬀects. "Hirs proposed a bulk-ﬂow theory for modeling turbulence in thin ﬁlm ﬂows.
In essence, the analysis seeks to relate the wall shear stress diﬀerences to the mean ﬂow velocity, where
the mean velocity conveys the meaning of an average velocity across the ﬁlm thickness."[4] The thin-ﬁlm
equations are, therefore, reduced so that all ﬂow- and pressure variables are bulk primitives.
83
==PAGE:96==
C.5
Bulk Flow Equations
In the following section, the bulk ﬂow equations will be derived based on the thin ﬁlm equation presented
in the last chapter. The procedure for obtaining the bulk ﬂow equations are straight forward - integrate
the thin ﬁlm equations over the thickness, use appropriate boundary conditions and reduce.

(assumption 10) and an ideal ﬂuid (assumption 5) the variables can be written as:

APPENDIX D
Derivation of the Perturbation of Equations
The following appendix is a supplement to chapter 5 of the main report. Please note, that not everything
will be further documented like ﬁgures etc. since they are already present in the main report. The
following section is based on derivation performed by the authors. Very little information is present in the
literature, so much of the work is based on assumptions, interpretations and fundamental understanding
og an annular seal.
D.1
Taylor Expansions
Certain expression in the bulk ﬂow equations cannot be handled in the present form, and successfully
form the perturbation equations where all terms can be equated to zero coeﬃcients to like power of
ϵ.
Therefore, a second order Taylor expansion of the non-dimensional relative velocities around the
unperturbed ﬂow (at equilibrium), and with insertion of equation D.3 results in.

D.2
Perturbation Equations
Seeking a ﬁrst order approximation to the dimensionless Bulk Flow equations derived in previous chap-
ter. It is assumed rotor performs small but ﬁnite motion about its equilibrium where the zeroth order
equations represents the unperturbed ﬂow (at equilibrium), and the ﬁrst order equations represent the
perturbed ﬂow due to the whirling rotor inside the seal. One generally assumes an expansion of the form
h(x, z, t) = h0(x, z, t) + ϵh1(x, z, t) + O(ϵ2) ⇔h = h0 + ϵh1 + O2
p(x, z, t) = p0(x, z, t) + ϵp1(x, z, t) + O(ϵ2) ⇔p = p0 + ϵp1 + O2
u(x, z, t) = u0(x, z, t) + ϵu1(x, z, t) + O(ϵ2) ⇔u = u0 + ϵu1 + O2
w(x, z, t) = w0(x, z, t) + ϵw1(x, z, t) + O(ϵ2) ⇔w = w0 + ϵw1 + O2
(D.3)

D.3

Under equilibrium condition (no eccentricity), the rotor remains centered. Thus there will be no change
of the zeroth order height or ﬂow in time, nor in the variables in the tangential direction. Thus.

==PAGE:105==
The ﬁrst order equations are 2D, as they all have derivatives in both the x and z-direction, and are
coupled. The motion will be either cylindrical or conical, the clearance function will, therefore, have
a simple harmonic variation in θ. This can be exploited to simplify the equation of motion from the
general 2D case (arbitrary motion) to the reduced 1D equations presented in the end of this section. The
clearance function is restated below, and new parameters are introduced to simplify the equation:
h1 = −xcos(z, t) cos(θ) −ysin(z, t) sin(θ)
(D.30)
Since the clearance function is a harmonic variation in θ, a simple harmonic variation in the dependent
variables can also be expected. The following separation of variables solution is therefore assumed for
the dependent perturbation variables. Subscript cos and sin indicate terms multiplied by cos and sin
respectively:
h1(z, t, θ) = −xcos(z, t) cos(θ) −y1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
w1(z, t, θ) = w1cos(z, t) cos(θ) + w1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
u1(z, t, θ) = u1cos(z, t) cos(θ) + u1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
p1(z, t, θ) = p1cos(z, t) cos(θ) + p1sin(z, t) sin(θ)
(D.31)
Equation 5.29 should be substituted into the ﬁrst order equations 5.18-5.20. Thus the continuity, axial
and circumferential ﬁrst order equation becomes

(D.34)
Terms multiplied by cos(θ) and sin(θ) should be collected. This results in six terms that are required
to be zero, since cos(θ) and sin(θ) cannot be 0 for θ ∈[0 : 2π]. The six terms form six real equations
and are only a function of z and t. The six equations can be reduced to three complex equations by
introducing the following complex representation of the dependent variables in the complex plane:
h1com(z, t) = −xcos(z, t) −jy1sin(z, t)
w1com(z, t) = w1cos(z, t) + jw1sin(z, t)
u1com(z, t) = u1cos(z, t) + ju1sin(z, t)
p1com(z, t) = p1cos(z, t) + jp1sin(z, t)
(D.35)
Application of the complex equation has been done as follows. First, from each pair of equations (cos,
sin from one equation), isolate respectively the xcos and ycos. Insert this into h1com(z, t) = −xcos(z, t) −
jy1sin(z, t). Then apply the other complex equations. The resulting system of equations becomes:
h0u1com −u0h1com + j R

APPENDIX E
Annular Seal Algorithm
The following appendix holds algorithms for prediction of dynamic forces in annular (liquid) seals. The
implementation is close to the presented pseudo code in ﬁgure 6.4.
Algorithm three has not been
introduced until this now, however is just a collection of the perturbation equations and friction factors.

Solver
34
[ output . f i e l d _ s o l u t i o n { i } , output . zeroth { i } ] = algorithm_2 ( data ) ;
35
36
%%% Frequency Reponse
Function
of
force
37
W0 = mean( output . zeroth { i }.W0) ;
38
Cd =
2∗data .DP/( data . rho ∗W0^2) ;
39
data . F0 = 2∗data . L∗data .R∗data .DP;
40
p1 = output . f i e l d _ s o l u t i o n { i } ( 5 , : ) ;
41
f r 1 { i }
= −(pi/Cd)
∗trapz ( output . z , real ( p1 ) ) ;
42
ftheta1 { i } = −(pi/Cd)
∗trapz ( output . z , imag( p1 ) ) ;
43
end
44
45
%% Least
Square
Fit
( frf_model = frf_num )
46
f o r
i = 1 : length ( f )
47
%%% A matrix
48
A1( i , : ) = −[1
f ( i ) −( f ( i ) ) ^ 2 ] ;
49
A2( i , : ) = [1 −f ( i ) ] ;
50
51
%%% b
matrix
52
b1 ( i ) = f r 1 { i };
53
b2 ( i ) = ftheta1 { i };
54
end
55
56
%%% Nondimensional
c o e f f i c i e n t s
57
x1 = ((A1’ ∗A1) ^(−1) ) ∗A1’ ∗b1 ’ ;
58
x2 = ((A2’ ∗A2) ^(−1) ) ∗A2’ ∗b2 ’ ;
59
output .Mnon = [ x1 (3)
0;
0 x1 (3) ] ;
60
output . Cnon = [ x2 (2)
x1 (2) ;
x1 (2)
x2 (2) ] ;
61
output . Knon = [ x1 (1)
x2 (1) ;
x2 (1)
x1 (1) ] ;
62
63
%%% Dimensional
c o e f f i c i e n t s
64
output .M = output .Mnon∗data . F0/( data . Cr∗data . omega^2) ;
65
output .K = output . Knon∗data . F0/data . Cr ;
66
output .C = output . Cnon∗data . F0/( data . Cr∗data . omega ) ;
67
M_ratio = output .M(1 ,1) ∗data . omega/ output .C(1 ,2) ;
68
K_ratio = output .K(1 ,2) /( output .C(1 ,1) ∗data . omega ) ;
69
end

E.3
Algorithm 2 - Perturbation Flow Solutions
1
function
[ sol , Information ] = algorithm_2 ( data )
2
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3
% FINITE DIFFERENCE SOLVER OF PERTURBATION FIELD VARIABLES %
4
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
5
% −−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−%
6
% Input :
Stuc
with
data
%
7
% Output :
Dimensionless
perturbation
solutions , W0
%
8
% −−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−%
9
% Creator : MSc.
Frank
Skovsgaard
%
10
% Creator : MSc.
Ari Lamhauge
%
11
% Version :
1 ,
2/3/2017
%
12
% −−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−%
13
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
14
15
%% Preprocessing
16
z = transpose ( linspace (0 ,1 , data .N) ) ;
step = z (2) −z (1) ;
17
N = data .N; NE = data .NE;
18
dA = zeros (N∗NE,N∗NE) ;
19
Ac = zeros (N∗NE,N∗NE) ;
20
21
%% Processing
22
%%% Node
2:n−1 ( Central
d i f f e r e n c e )
23
dfdz = [−1 0
1]/(2∗step ) ;
24
f o r
j = NE+1:N∗NE−NE;
25
dof = [ j−NE, j , j+NE] ;
26
dA( j , dof ) = dA( j , dof ) + dfdz ;
27
end
28
29
%%% Node 1 ( Forward
d i f f e r e n c e )
30
dfdz = [ −3/(2∗step )
2/ step
−1/(2∗step ) ] ;
31
dof = [ 1 ,NE+1,2∗NE+1];
32
f o r
j = 1:NE
33
dA( j , dof+j −1) = dA( j , dof+j −1) + dfdz ;
34
end
35
36
%%% Node n ( Backwards
d i f f e r e n c e )
37
dfdz = [1/(2∗step ) −2/step
3/(2∗step ) ] ;
38
dof = [N∗NE−2∗NE,N∗NE−NE,N∗NE] ;
39
f o r
j = 1:NE
40
dA(N∗NE−j +1,dof−j +1) = dA(N∗NE−j +1,dof−j +1) + dfdz ;
41
end
42
43
%%% I t e r a t i o n
due
to
f r i c t i o n
44
r e l a x = 0 . 5 ;
45
u0_old = ones (N, 1 ) ;
46
u0_new = ones (N, 1 ) +0.5;
47
W0_old = 47;
48
W0_new = 46;
49
p0_old = ones (N, 1 ) ;
50
p0_new = ones (N, 1 ) +0.5;
51
52
while
( max( abs( u0_old−u0_new) ) > data . e r r
| |
max( abs( p0_old−p0_new) ) > data . e r r
)
53
%%% Clear Ac ( every
i t e r a t i o n )
54
Ac = zeros (NE∗N,NE∗N) ;
55
56
%%%% Update
c i r c u m f e r i e c i a l
flow
57
u0_old = u0_new ;
58
p0_old = p0_new ;
59
W0_old = W0_new;
60
61
%%% Constants
( Local / g l o b a l
matrices )
62
f o r
j = 1:N;
63
dof = [NE∗j −(NE−1) :NE∗j ] ;
64
[ Al , bl ] = algorithm_3 ( data , u0_old ( j ) ,W0_old) ;
65
Ac( dof , dof ) = Ac( dof , dof ) + Al
;
66
b( dof , 1 ) = bl ;
67
end
68
69
%%% A matrix
70
A = dA + Ac ;
71
97
==PAGE:110==
72
%%% Zeroth
order
s o l u t i o n s
only
73
dof_zeroth = sort ( [ 1 :NE:N∗NE 2:NE:N∗NE] ) ;
74
A = A( dof_zeroth , dof_zeroth ) ;
75
b = b( dof_zeroth , 1 ) ;
76
77
%%%% Boundary
conditions
78
b (1) = data . u0 ; A( 1 , : ) = 0; A(1 ,1) = 1 ;
79
b(2∗N) = −(1−data . xi_e ) /2∗data . h0 ( end ) ; A(2∗N, : ) = 0 ; A(2∗N,2∗N) = 1;
80
81
%%%% Solution
82
A = sparse (A) ;
83
b = sparse (b) ;
84
s o l = A\b ;
85
s o l = f u l l ( s o l ) ;
86
87
%%% I t e r a t i o n
88
p0_new = ( s o l ( 2 : 2 : 2 ∗N) ) ;
89
u0_new = ( s o l ( 1 : 2 : 2 ∗N) ) ;
90
u0_new = u0_new∗r e l a x + u0_old∗(1−r e l a x ) ;
91
p0_new = p0_new∗r e l a x + p0_old∗(1−r e l a x ) ;
92
93
i f
( data . Rz0 == 0)
94
W0_new = sqrt (( data .DP/(p0_new (1)+(1+data . xi ) /2∗data . h0 ^2) ) / data . rho ) ;
95
W0_new = W0_new∗r e l a x + W0_old∗(1−r e l a x ) ;
96
else
97
W0_new = data .mu∗data . Rz0/(2∗data . Cr∗data . rho ) ;
98
end
99
end
100
s o l 1 = s o l ;
101
102
%%% Clear Ac ( every
i t e r a t i o n )
103
Ac = zeros (NE∗N,NE∗N) ;
104
105
%%% Constants
( Local / g l o b a l
matrices )
106
f o r
j = 1:N;
107
dof = [NE∗j −(NE−1) :NE∗j ] ;
108
[ Al , bl ] = algorithm_3 ( data , u0_new( j ) ,W0_new) ;
109
Ac( dof , dof ) = Ac( dof , dof ) + Al
;
110
b( dof , 1 ) = bl ;
111
end
112
113
%%% A matrix
114
A = dA + Ac ;
115
116
%%% F i r s t
order
s o l u t i o n s
only
117
d o f _ f i r s t = sort ( [ 3 :NE:N∗NE 4:NE:N∗NE 5:NE:N∗NE] ) ;
118
A = A( dof_first , d o f _ f i r s t ) ;
119
b = b( dof_first , 1 ) ;
120
121
%%% Boundary
conditions
122
%%%% u1 (0) = 0
123
A( 2 , : ) = 0 ; A(2 ,2) = 1;
b (3) = 0;
124
125
%%%% p1 (0) + k1∗w1(0) = 0;
126
A( 3 , : ) = 0 ; A(3 ,3) = 1; A(3 ,3 −2) = (1+data . xi ) / data . h0 ;
b (3) = 0 ;
127
128
%%%% p1 (1) + k2∗w1(1) = 0;
129
A(3∗N, : ) = 0; A(3∗N,3∗N) = 1; A(3∗N,3∗N−2) = (1−data . xi_e ) / data . h0 ;
130
b(3∗N) = 0;
131
132
%%%% Solution
133
A = sparse (A) ;
b = sparse (b) ;
134
s o l = A\b ;
s o l = f u l l ( s o l ) ;
s o l 2 = s o l ;
135
136
%% Postprocessing
137
Information .W0 = W0_new;
138
temp ( 1 , : ) = s o l 1 ( 1 : 2 : end ) ;
139
temp ( 2 , : ) = s o l 1 ( 2 : 2 : end ) ;
140
temp ( 3 , : ) = s o l 2 ( 1 : 3 : end ) ;
141
temp ( 4 , : ) = s o l 2 ( 2 : 3 : end ) ;
142
temp ( 5 , : ) = s o l 2 ( 3 : 3 : end ) ;
143
s o l = temp ;
144
end

Algorithm 3 - Perturbation Equations of Motion

function
[A, g ] = algorithm_3 ( data , u0 ,W0)
2
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

% PERTURBATION EQUATIONS OF MOTION ON MATRIX FORM

s t r u c t
with
data ,
non−dim pre−s w i r l
and
a i x a l
flow %

% Output :
Constant−Matrix A and
vector

% −−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−−%

%%% Allocation
16

18
%%% Gradient
of
h
19

20

%% Friction
Model −Here Moody
22

23

25

27

a3 = 10^6/Rz0 ;
29

30

%%%% Relative
V e l o c t i t y
33

34
ur0 = sqrt ( data . w0^2+b^2∗(u0−1)^2) ;
35

%%% Zeroth
Order
Friction
C o e f f i c i e n t s
37

38
Br0 = a2r / data . h0 + a3 /( data . h0∗ur0 ) ;
39
f s 0 = a1∗(1+Bs0 ^(1/3) ) ;
40
f r 0 = a1∗(1+Br0 ^(1/3) ) ;
41
42
zeroth . f s 0 = f s 0 ;
43
zeroth . f r 0 = f r 0 ;
44
zeroth . us0 = us0 ;
45
zeroth . ur0 = ur0 ;
46

47
zeroth . sigmar0 = f r 0 ∗( data . L/data . Cr) ;
48
49
%%% F i r s t
Order
Friction
C o e f f i c i e n t s
50
Bs0 = a2s / data . h0 + ( a3 /( data . h0∗us0 ) ) ;
51
Br0 = a2r / data . h0 + ( a3 /( data . h0∗ur0 ) ) ;
52
bs = −1/(3∗Bs0 ) ∗(1−a1/ f s 0 ) ∗( a3 /( data . h0∗us0 ) ) ;
53
br = −1/(3∗Br0 ) ∗(1−a1/ f r 0 ) ∗( a3 /( data . h0∗ur0 ) ) ;
54
55
f i r s t . A1z = 1/(2∗data . h0 ^3) ∗data . L/data . Cr ∗( . . .
56
+f s 0 ∗us0 ∗(1+1/3∗(1−a1/ f s 0 ) ) . . .
57
+f r 0 ∗ur0 ∗(1+1/3∗(1−a1/ f r 0 ) )
) ;
58
59
f i r s t . A1x = 1/(2∗data . h0 ^2) ∗data . L/data . Cr ∗( . . .
60
+f s 0 ∗us0∗u0∗(1+1/3∗(1−a1/ f s 0 ) ) . . .
61
+f r 0 ∗ur0 ∗(u0−1)∗(1+1/3∗(1−a1/ f r 0 ) )
) ;
62
63
f i r s t . A2z = b^2/(2∗data . h0 ^2) ∗data . L/ data . Cr ∗( . . .
64
+f s 0 /us0∗u0 ∗( bs+1) . . .
65
+f r 0 /ur0 ∗(u0−1)∗( br+1) ) ;

68
+f s 0 /us0 ∗( us0^2+b^2∗u0 ^2∗( bs+1)) . . .
69
+f r 0 /ur0 ∗( ur0^2+b^2∗(u0−1)^2∗( br+1))
) ;

f i r s t . A3z = 1/(2∗data . h0 ) ∗data . L/data . Cr ∗( . . .
99

+f s 0 /us0 ∗( us0^2+data . w0^2∗( bs+1)) . . .

−1/ data . h0^2∗dh0dz
) ;
